B0091Q82L8|4.0|'Live at Hull 1970' documents The Who's performance from the night after the 'Live at Leeds' show, and as such it may be somewhat redundant for anyone who already owns the legendary Leeds set in full. The Who were not a band whose performances varied much from show to show, as can be heard on this recording. In fact, Entwistle's bass on the first four songs here are taken directly from the night before's performance--due to the fact that it wasn't captured on the original soundboard recording--evidence of the extreme similarity between the two nights.<br /><br />Still, for the hardcore Who fan, this is a high-quality, \\"warts-and-all\\" release that shows the band at the peak of their powers, with an energy and vigor that few bands at the time, or any time, could match. Keith Moon is an absolute maniac here, pummeling his drums seemingly into splinters--the periodic crackle you'll notice is Moon repeatedly hitting his overhead mic by accident. In some ways, Keith's performance here is even more impressive than on 'Leeds' (or 'Isle of Wight' from later in the year), and that alone warrants a purchase for fans of his. Townshend's peerless rhythm guitar wizardry, Entwistle's precision bass, and Daltrey's acrobatic vocals hold their own as well, refusing to be buried beneath Moon's insane bombastic clangor.<br /><br />Like 'Leeds,' 'Live at Hull' showcases the band performing 'Tommy' in it's entirety, and the rest of the concert is identical as well (with nearly identical renditions of the songs), with one exception. Rather than the (amazing) first-set encore of \\"Magic Bus,\\" the set closer here is a 15-plus minute, ultra-jammed-out version of \\"My Generation\\" that not only is more powerful than the Leeds version, imo, it's without question the best, most visceral I've heard, and yet another reason to own this set.<br /><br />'Live at Hull,' while not absolutely essential for the casual Who fan, is definitely recommended for the more serious fan--especially those of Keith Moon--and for those who only own the one-disc version of 'Leeds,' as there are enough differences between the two to warrant a purchase, although the sound quality, while still very good, is not quite on the same level as 'Leeds' or 'Isle of Wight.' Still, it's nearly two hours of one of rock's most important, most influential bands performing their rock opera masterpiece impeccably, with an energy that's rarely been topped throughout the history of rock. And that's reason enough to own this two-disc set in my book.

0765316978|5.0|\\"The Last Colony\\" returns to John Perry and Jane Sagan, retired from the military, as they colonize a new world.<br /><br />The book is about secrets and peace.  Readers of the first two books will know that secrets abound among the Colonial Union.  However, in the first two books, the secrets were largely incidental.  Yes, they played a major role in the plot, but the protagonists were happy to live their lives without confronting the secrets, so they weren't a big part of the story.<br /><br />\\"The Last Colony\\" gives secrets a front seat.  Pretty much every major event in the book comes as the result of a secret or its revelation.  Characters debate about the ethics of keeping secrets.  Sometimes they fall on the side of keeping secrets, sometimes on the side of revealing secrets, and sometimes on the side of allowing better choices even without full information.  As a result, there is a rich discourse on honesty and its discontents.  A side effect of this is that there are tons of exciting plot twists in the book, and all of them feel natural and well integrated into the story.<br /><br />The theme of peace comes about through a larger debate on that one big secret that you learn about at the end of \\"Ghost Brigades.\\"  When a group is trying to push for peace, how can people tell that they're genuine and equitable?  Where do a person's loyalties lie: with their country or their conscience?  Is there such a thing as a just war?  What about a just enforcement mechanism for peace?  Where is the room for idealism in a harsh universe?  John Perry, as an antiwar soldier who dislikes groups that threaten humanity and who dislikes warlike and secret elements of humanity, brings these questions to the forefront.<br /><br />One thing that I liked a lot about \\"Old Man's War\\" and \\"Ghost Brigades\\" was that they felt finished after the end.  To be sure, there were lots of things to come in future books and a lot left unsaid, but the significant events of the book felt concluded.  In \\"The Last Colony,\\" there is one very salient issue that comes up a few times throughout the book that is never satisfactorily addressed.  Perry event talks with another character, and that character says that they haven't given the issue much thought, but then they don't give the issue more thought later on.  I could accept that if I felt like it would be addressed in a future book, but since \\"The Last Colony\\" finished things up, it just feels like a loose end.<br /><br />That said, the ending was superb.  The series is nicely framed, I feel good about the state of the universe, and the goodness doesn't feel like an artificial Hollywood ending.. Why?  Because, generally speaking, we go to the cinema to ESCAPE our own realities, not get sucked up in them once again.  Thus, for all the good things that \\"Flight\\" accomplishes, it is just too \\"grounded\\" to ever real produce the kind of emotional response needed to make it truly great.<br /><br />For a basic plot summary (containing some minor spoilers), \\"Flight\\" sees pilot Whip Whitaker (Denzel Washington) perform an incredible feat of flying by landing a large commercial airliner that is literally falling apart around him.  The only problem?  Mr. Whitaker is an alcoholic who also uses cocaine to \\"rev himself back up\\" and continue flying.  When both substances are found on his toxicology report after the crash, Whip embarks on a personal journey that ultimately ends with him confronting his own personal demons.<br /><br />There are some things that this film does very well.  The direction from Robert Zemeckis is sound, Denzel turns in a great performance, and the airplane sequences are absolutely captivating.  The plot and auxiliary characters are also just good enough to make it a solid effort despite my biggest critique:<br /><br />I have never viewed a film so down-to-earth and utterly devoid of an ounce of contrived drama.  Usually that is a good thing (as so many films go the opposite route), but in this case it just makes for a flick that doesn't really arouse any deep emotions or feelings towards the on-screen proceedings. Wild wolves are generally timid around humans. Wolves usually try to avoid contact with people, to the point of even abandoning their kills when an approaching human is detected, but there are several reported circumstances in whichwolves have been recorded to act aggressively toward humans.    Compared to other carnivorous mammals known to attack humans in general, the frequency with which wolves have been recorded to kill or prey on people is much lower, indicating that though potentially dangerous, wolves are among the least threatening for their size and predatory potential.[1]    [...]

B008JFUUIA|3.0|I see so many movies every month I couldn't possibly write a review for every single one. When I get around to putting pen to paper, I have to think if I have anything to say about a film and then decide which to write about. Oddly, every time I think about writing reviews for the current crop of films, I forget that I have seen \\"Pitch Perfect\\", the new film starring Anna Kendrick (\\"Twilight\\", \\"Up in the Air\\") as the newest member of an all-female college acapela club.<br /><br />I'm not sure why that is. \\"Pitch\\" is a perfectly enjoyable way to spend a few hours. A lot of it works, there are many laughs, some of the music is very good. But I think that for almost every good thing, there is an equally noticeable problem.<br /><br />Kendrick, ready to break free from brooding vampires, headlines as Beca, a brooding freshman attending Borden University. She is going to college because her dad is a professor there and insists she give it a try before heading to Los Angeles to make it big as a DJ peddling her mash-ups. She soon discovers the Borden Bellas, an all-female acapela group fighting their way back to Nationals after a disastrous performance the previous year. Beca joins the group and pushes her mash-ups; she is all about trying something new, something different.<br />The new head of the group, Aubrey (Anna Camp, TV's \\"The Good Wife\\", \\"Mad Men\\", \\"The Help\\") wants to maintain the group's traditional ways and songs. Her right hand, Chloe (Brittany Snow, \\"Hairspray (2007)\\", TV's \\"Harry's Law\\") is more on the fence, but gives in to her friend's pressure to remain tried and true.<br /><br />Because of the Bella's recent performance and graduating seniors, they have to start from scratch and bring in an entirely new team. They find a black lesbian, a promiscuous girl, a shy, demure Asian girl, a couple of less distinctive and less interesting girls and Fat Amy (Rebel Wilson, \\"Bridesmaids\\"). In other words, they find characters defined by a single characteristic, making them stereotypes. With one exception.<br /><br />Rebel Wilson is a stand-out as the self-proclaimed Fat Amy. Her jokes and funny bits zig zag back and forth between self-deprecating humor and jokes others make about her. You can tell the jokes made by others sting a little, but she is also used to them, aware of them, and has learned to recover from them very quickly. And her self-deprecating humor is a form of armor, protection from the mean-spiritedness of others.<br /><br />The Bella's rivals, an all-male acapela group, are currently number #1. They do modern stuff and never repeat a song, leading them to enjoy a lot of success. Jesse (Skylar Astin) and Benji (Ben Platt), two roommates and new students, both want to join, but Jesse is chosen. He also has eyes for Beca.<br /><br />You can probably connect many of the dots and guess many of the jokes.<br /><br />Elizabeth Banks, one of the film's producers, appears with John Michael Higgins (TV's \\"Happily Divorced\\", \\"Best in Show\\", \\"The Ugly Truth\\") as the television hosts of the Nationals. These roles are pretty much the exact role Higgins has played many times before, in \\"Best of Show\\", \\"For Your Consideration\\", usually with Jane Lynch in a film written and directed by Christopher Guest. It was a funny bit the first time, but each time it is repeated it becomes less interesting and even a little annoying. Now, it just appears tired and recycled.<br /><br />This brings me to my big complaint about \\"Perfect\\". The filmmakers seem to reach the limit of their abilities after the few good things and fill in the blanks with recycled material. The bits with Higgins and Banks stand out like a sore thumb. And because she is one of the Producers, it seems like she could've, should've said \\"this seems very familiar. How can we take this somewhere new?\\" But she didn't and it just seems so similar. There should almost be a clich&eacute; named for these characters. The snarky co-hosts. Stanley Tucci and Toby Jones even did avariation on this bit, but with more sarcasm, in \\"The Hunger Games\\". The additional level of snarkiness makes it a fitting addition to the futuristic survival tale. It makes it a little more interesting and brings a slightly different slant to the rote idea.<br /><br />Also, there seem to be a lot of missed opportunities. Beca and Jesse both go to work at the college radio station, a story idea meant to force them to spend a lot of time together, forcing them to deal with their growing attraction. But the station is run by a hunky British DJ. (Freddie Stroma, the \\"Harry Potter\\" films) who doesn't really seem to serve any purpose what so ever. He doesn't even do the most clich&eacute;d thing imaginable and cause Jesse to become jealous.<br /><br />All of the supporting characters amount to little more than stereotypes. There is so much opportunity to explore a little more of these characters that it seems a shame everything is reduced to a simple one-liner. Very often, the one-liner is repeated.<br /><br />The music is quite good. Kendrick and her female counterparts seem ready to break out of their mold at any point, held back only by Aubrey's insistence on tradition. There are a number of occasions when they aren't competing, allowing Beca more leeway and she breaks out into a modern song. But every time they go to competition, it is the same old, same old. The male group, the more polished performers, more willing and able to take chances and perform new material, seem more modern and daring. Their songs also provide a nice counterpoint to the more formal, old-fashioned arrangements of the Bellas. You know the Bellas will eventually change their routing and when they do, the performance is pretty great.<br /><br />As good as the music is, it doesn't really do anything better than a good episode of \\"Glee\\". When the television show if firing on all cylinders, the musical performances are memorable, the characters explore real emotions and real problems, and this might provide some genuinely moving moments. Granted, this seems to be happening less frequently this season, but it does happen.<br /><br />Ultimately, \\"Pitch\\" doesn't separate itself enough from a good episode of \\"Glee\\" to make it worth spending $13 for a full-price ticket. Bargain matinee, sure.tion on this bit, but with more sarcasm, in \\"The Hunger Games\\". The additional level of snarkiness makes it a fitting addition to the futuristic survival tale. B005LAII8A|4.0|Films involving the barest mention of time travel are usually problematic. Inevitably, a bunch of arm-chair critics will begin to debate all of the ways the time-travel doesn't and can't work, no doubt worrying about their congealing breakfast burrito in the process.<br /><br />\\"Looper\\", the new film starring Joseph Gordon - Levitt as Joe, a 'Looper' who kills the mob's enemies, seems to embrace all of the possible problems and work with them making them more real in the process.<br /><br />It's 2074 and time travel has now been banned. But the mob still uses this outlawed technology to send their enemies back from the future to 2074, into the hands of the Loopers who are waiting to execute them. Joe has one standing order; as soon as the victim appears, he is to shoot them and then collect his payment, silver bars attached to the hit's back. In this way, the mob essentially erases all traces of their future enemies. And Joe is well-aware that he will one day be expected to kill himself. When this happens, he can take the gold bars attached to his older self, and try to live a few years as well as possible. But he isn't looking forward to this day.<br /><br />When Old Joe (Bruce Willis) arrives, he catches his younger self by surprise and escapes. During the intervening years, Old Joe has learned a lot about Cid, the head of the mob. Now that he has been sent back to 2074, he wants to find the younger Cid and correct a lot of bad things before they happen by killing the future mob boss. But young Cid is an orphan, living with and cared for by Sara (Emily Blunt), who will do anything to protect him. Younger Joe is unaware of all of this. He just knows he has to find his older self and avoid the hit men sent by his boss, Abe (Jeff Daniels), the local mob boss.<br /><br />Written and directed by Rian Johnson (\\"Brick\\", \\"The Brothers' Bloom\\"), \\"Looper\\" is a well-made action/ sci-fi hybrid. Both of Johnson's earlier films were good, but flawed. With \\"Looper\\", he proves the adage 'third times the charm.'<br /><br />The sci-fi elements work because they are almost downplayed. Kansas City in 2074 doesn't look a lot different from what it probably looks like now. The cars are a little different, the streets are dirtier and filled with more homeless, the guns are a little different, but with the exception of the whole 'time travel' thing, not much seems to have changed. Because Johnson downplays the elements of the 'future', the future seems more real. Because everything seems more real, our inquisitive minds spend less time thinking about the science and looking for loopholes and we just sit back and enjoy the ride.<br /><br />Johnson uses an interesting, fairly uncommon narrative technique to get us involved in the story. We spend a significant amount of time with Joe (Gordon - Levitt), getting to know everything about the routines of his life. Then, when Old Joe (Willis) shows up, we spend some time flashing back to his life and what he has learned. After we catch up, the story continues and we start to follow them both. Most films would follow both leads, alternating back and forth, dividing our time and attention. It's a nice way for us to get familiar and comfortable with each before launching into a new phase with both.<br /><br />Gordon - Levitt is very good as Joe. You get the sense he doesn't really enjoy his job, but given the state of the World, his job allows him a comfort most people don't have. So he does his job willingly and well and turns to Suzie (Piper Perabo), a stripper, for solace and companionship. And he seems to sleepwalk through his life, going from one point to another without much joy or sense of accomplishment. But his survival depends on playing a game and doing whatever possible to keep his bosses happy. And he also knows he will eventually be faced with killing himself. This leads him to face everything with a mild case of skepticism. It is a nice way to add maturity to his character and give him depth.<br /><br />And the alternative is even worse, so he does what he is good at.<br /><br />Willis' Old Joe doesn't have a lot of dialogue and spends most of the film in action. This allows us to fill in some of the blanks as we watch the flashback illustrating his later life. It also allows us to learn with his character. Yes, he has already witnessed a lot, but when he returns to 2074, he learns new information and has to contend with himself as a younger, stronger, less mature man. He has to assimilate the new information before he makes a mistake. In these moments, we watch how he reacts, we watch as he learns and we learn with him.<br /><br />The flashback illustrating Joe's life from 2074 forward is particularly interesting and adds a lot to the overall story.<br /><br />Emily Blunt plays Suzie, a woman who cares for and tries to tame a young boy named Cid. She plays the role with such earnestness, you almost forget about the sci-fi universe she inhabits, even when 'sci-fi' things are happening. She has adopted this child, she is his mother, and she won't let anything happen to him.<br /><br />Jeff Daniels plays Abe, the local crime boss sent back from the future to keep an eye on things. It is a nice a-typical performance for the actor. He appears to be the same affable guy, but beneath all of the good-nature lives a very deep level of menace and danger. Every moment he appears on screen you feel he could snap at any point. When this pattern is established, we simply wait for the moment. This anticipation creates a high level of suspense.<br /><br />Paul Dano is also good as Seth, Joe's friend and a fellow Looper. His story provides a nice illustration of what happens to 'Loopers' when their time has come and helps us realize just how desperate Joe is to avoid the same fate.<br /><br />\\"Looper\\" is surprisingly good and very entertaining.r /><br />And the alternative is even worse, so he does what he is good at.<br /><br />Willis' Old Joe doesn't have a lot of dialogue and spends most of the film in action. 0547564651|4.0|In this seminal book, Paul Tough suggests that for success in life, children need not so much an early start on cognitive skills as help managing their impulses and ability to focus.  To grasp how children succeed, we must look at the parent-child relationship, which is the crucible for much of a child's developmental trajectory.  It is also important to consider how kids acquire skills when they do, and how character is formed.<br /><br />Key obstacles can be adverse events in childhood--trauma and related stress, also problems with attachment and nurturance as it mitigates a child's experience of stress.  Researchers have created an \\"adversity score\\" that accurately predicts how well a child will cope in later life.<br /><br />IQ scores have been found to predict not success in college but rather scores on standardized tests.  Indeed, IQ scores have been found to change over time. Educators working with socioeconomically disadvantaged children have found that college performance is more accurately predicted by grade point average, which reflects not simply academic skills but also self-discipline, perseverance, and the ability to delay gratification--in short, character.<br /><br />(Tough notes parenthetically that affluence negative impacts character formation: \\"for a young person to build character [he must] attempt something where there is a serious possibility of failure\\" [p. 85]).<br /><br />By studying children who persist in college and receive a degree, and comparing them with peers who dropped out earlier in their academic careers, educators have identified character traits important for success.  One list includes grit, self-control, zest, social intelligence, gratitude, optimism, and curiosity.  Youngsters need a mindset that welcomes challenge more than approbation.<br /><br />The book is full of fascinating tidbits from recent research into the learning process.  Did you know, for example, about the confirmation bias?  Asked to guess the rule underlying a string of numbers, most people will err by picking a too-restrictive rule, producing examples that fit the rule rather than searching instead for an example that breaks the rule. \\"To win the game is to guess strings of numbers that would prove your beloved hypothesis wrong--and that is something each of us is constitutionally driven to avoid\\" (p. 139).<br /><br />Other nuggets.  The number of college dropouts is on the rise in part because of undermatching of students and institutions of learning.  Also, true masters of a sport or skill are pessimists, not optimists, in assessing the likely outcome of a particular strategy (the examples given involve chess).<br /><br />This book is perhaps most valuable for the insights it affords into parenting practices.  Parents need to nurture their youngsters primarily when the children are stressed.  Kids need the opportunity to overcome failure and to learn from it.  Tough describes a number of pioneering programs now yielding positive results, including therapy that teaches parenting skills to improve wellness and attachment and to develop executive function and self-regulation in children.<br /><br />This thoughtful, readable view of human development and our flawed American approach to public education is a welcome antidote to the national preoccupation with rote learning in children at ever younger ages.ng a string of numbers, most people will err by picking a too-restrictive rule, producing examples that fit the rule rather than searching instead for an example that breaks the rule. \\"To win the game is to guess strings of numbers that would prove your beloved hypothesis wrong--and that is something each of us is constitutionally driven to avoid\\" (p. 139).<br /><br />Other nuggets. B0087ITC9C|4.0|***This review contains spoilers, but nothing that isn't shown in the trailer. If you want to go in knowing nothing, stop reading now.***<br /><br />Denzel Washington rarely disappoints, and his role in Flight is one of his most interesting performances in years. Unlike many of his roles, this one casts doubts about whether Washington's character is a hero or a villain.<br /><br />If you haven't seen any reviews of the film, you might think that Flight is going to feature a lot of action, but the action is over once we experience the crash sequence. This is very much a drama.<br /><br />The opening scenes show Captain Whip Whitaker (Washington) in bed with a flight attendant. He's been awake all night and he's drunk and high on cocaine. That's not exactly the best preparation for work when you are going to be entrusted with the lives of everyone on board a plane. Would you be happy if you knew that your pilot was in such a state?<br /><br />Whitaker feels the need for another drink before the plane takes off, and drinks vodka without anyone noticing. When he takes his seat next to the co-pilot, Ken, his behavior appears a little odd. Whitaker orders coffee and Aspirin and takes off as though nothing is wrong. Ken's suspicions heighten when Whitaker exceeds the recommended speed while climbing to escape bad weather.<br /><br />If you remember the crash in Cast Away, you'll know that Robert Zemeckis knows how to portray such an event effectively. In Flight, the crash is even more terrifying. If you are nervous about flying, this won't help you overcome it. The plane starts to fall apart at 30,000 feet, but Whitaker remains calm, although everyone else is panicking. In  one of the best scenes in the film, he takes the aircraft into a controlled roll and flies upside down to recover from an uncontrolled dive, before landing it in a field. Six of the 102 people on board are killed.<br /><br />That all happens during the first 30 minutes of the film. Whitaker is apparently a hero, and we later learn that what he did was something that other pilots were unable to duplicate in simulated tests. So why isn't that the end of the story?<br /><br />Someone has to be blamed for the crash.<br /><br />A parallel story shows Nicole (Kelly Reilly), who is addicted to drugs. She meets Whitaker in the corridor of a hospital, when both sneak out to smoke a cigarette. Addiction is a key theme in the film and the two develop a kind of bond, recognizing similar character traits in each other.<br /><br />The remainder of the story is a character study, and shows how Whitaker deals with life after the crash. Addictions to drugs and alcohol have affected some of the most important relationships in his life. We see him struggle to face that fact and the impact of the decisions he makes.<br /><br />Whitaker isn't completely free to confront his personal demons as he is being investigated to determine whether he was at fault during the crash. We see him prepare for a hearing, which is not a trial in court, but could lead to him being found responsible for the six deaths, and ultimately result in him serving a life sentence. His preparation is aided by Charlie (Bruce Greenwood), a former pilot and colleague, and Hugh (Don Cheadle), who is a lawyer. Both give strong performances in small supporting roles. The most colorful character in the film is Whitaker's friend, Harling Mays (John Goodman). If Goodman had played The Dude in The Big Lebowski, he would have looked something like this.<br /><br />Melissa Leo does a good job as Ellen Block, who leads the investigation into Whitaker's performance. B006YTYIXG|4.0|Soulfly's eighth full-length record, 2012's \\"Enslaved,\\" continues on the same angry, death-metal-influenced, riff-oriented road that frontman Max Cavalera and the Gang first started down on 2005's \\"Dark Ages.\\" But this album may very darn well be the strongest Soulfly recording of the past several years (it is their best effort since 2004's \\"Prophecy\\"). This is true because \\"Enslaved\\" is a very consistent, front-to-back listen, as even the weakest tracks on here pack in plenty of positively excellent riffs. And you will also, most likely, be treated to brutal drumming and vocals, as well. And, as was the case with the above-mentioned \\"Dark Ages,\\" and 2008's \\"Conquer\\" and 2010's \\"Omen,\\" \\"Enslaved\\" is a loosely-based concept album. The occasionally political lyrics -- see \\"Intervention\\" and \\"Treachery\\" for two examples -- add a deeper meaning to the songs, and convey a strong, convincing sense of nihilism, anarchy, and Armageddon.<br /><br />The set opens with \\"Resistance,\\" a forebodingly dark instrumental intro with a martial drum beat and ominous guitar noise. This flows perfectly into \\"World Scum,\\" which aside from a mid-tempo bass and drum intro, is a chaotic blitzkrieg. Grindcore-esque vocals -- including some monstrous guest bellows from Cattle Decapitation's Travis Ryan -- and grindcore-inspired, Dying Fetus-esque blast beats trade-off with Max's signature hardcore-shout. (The tune sneaks in some anthemic, \\"Chaos A.D.\\"-era Sepultura-like gang shouts of the title phrase.) And the next song, \\"Intervention,\\" might have a slow, doomy-flavored breakdown near the end, but it, too, is a blisteringly fast track. Here, great, pounding guitar and bass riffs (which thunder through the mix) give-way to ferocious, racing tremolo picking.<br /><br />\\"Gladiator\\" is a turbo-charged thrasher with double-kick-drum-backed buzzsaw riffage. A piercing, sweeping, technical melodic guitar solo and catchy, call-and-response vocal part also crop up later on in the song. \\"Legions\\" continues this frenetic pace, backing Eighties-era thrash-esque speedster picking with deft thrash beats, and topping it all off with some unorthodox, whammy-bar-drenched soloing. \\"American Steel\\" is another acrobatic guitar workout, one featuring massively crunching, machine-gun-fast riffing, some catchy wah-wah licks, and a pretty, colorful acoustic outro. \\"Redemption Of Man By God,\\" which features guest vocals from Devildriver's Dez Fafara, is backed by big, bone-crunching licks and busy, blasting drums.<br /><br />\\"Treachery\\" is a breakneck attack that evokes old-school Sepultura, and \\"Plata O Plomo,\\" which is sung entirely in Portuguese, and offsets its grinding blasts with a couple of splashes of gorgeous flamenco guitar strumming, is another throwback to Max's old days. \\"Chains\\" is slightly more mid-tempo, but still super heavy -- it has a deep, brooding groove, gut-punching riffs, catchy staccato vocals, and a fiery, searing guitar solo. The set concludes with \\"Revengeance,\\" which is a bass-heavy/driven closer with thundering bass lines (including a grumbling mini-bass solo) that compliment the tune's rapid-fire drumming very nicely.<br /><br />\\"Enslaved\\" is easily the angriest, darkest, fastest, heaviest, and most technically-impressive Soulfly offering to date. It might not be a particularly groundbreaking affair, but it does definitely make for numerous extremely enjoyable, enthralling, and fully-satisfying listening sessions.nues this frenetic pace, backing Eighties-era thrash-esque speedster picking with deft thrash beats, and topping it all off with some unorthodox, whammy-bar-drenched soloing. \\"American Steel\\" is another acrobatic guitar workout, one featuring massively crunching, machine-gun-fast riffing, some catchy wah-wah licks, and a pretty, colorful acoustic outro. \\"Redemption Of Man By God,\\" which features guest vocals from Devildriver's Dez Fafara, is backed by big, bone-crunching licks and busy, blasting drums.<br /><br />\\"Treachery\\" is a breakneck attack that evokes old-school Sepultura, and \\"Plata O Plomo,\\" which is sung entirely in Portuguese, and offsets its grinding blasts with a couple of splashes of gorgeous flamenco guitar strumming, is another throwback to Max's old days. \\"Chains\\" is slightly more mid-tempo, but still super heavy -- it has a deep, brooding groove, gut-punching riffs, catchy staccato vocals, and a fiery, searing guitar solo. B008YKRRH2|5.0|The final version of Anton Bruckner's Eighth Symphony was premiered in 1890.<br />He was still working on his Ninth Symphony when he died in 1896. It was premiered in 1903.<br />His circle of students and friends - Franz Schalk, Ferdinand Loewe and Gustav Mahler - dominated German and Austrian music in the decades leading up to World War I.<br /><br />To the generation of conductors who began their careers in the period 1890-1910, the music of Bruckner was contemporary music.<br />Six of them left us recordings of at least one Bruckner Symphony:<br /><br />- Bruno Walter (1876-1962)................Conducting debut: 1894<br />- Carl Schuricht (1880-1967)....................Debut: 1900<br />- Oskar Fried (1871-1941)..........................Debut: 1904 (began conducting late, at age 33)<br />- Hermann Abendroth (1883-1956) ................. Debut:1905<br />- Otto Klemperer (1885-1973).......................Debut: 1905<br />- Wilhelm Furtwangler (1886-1954)...................Debut: 1906<br /><br />Three of them lived long enough to record in Stereo (beginning in 1955).<br /><br />Their conducting styles were different from each other (and changed as they aged).<br />Nevertheless, all five had strong personalities which they effectively communicated to an orchestra.<br />I still listen to these recordings with pleasure, which is more than I can say for dozens of faceless conductors who came afterward (no names!).<br /><br />[hint: for ease of navigation, read the review though to the end, then come back and click on the links.]<br /><br />Of the conductors on this list, Otto Klemperer was the first and the last to commercially record a Bruckner Symphony.<br />He recorded the Adagio 3rd movement of the 8th Symphony with the Berlin State Opera Orchestra in 1924: [[ASIN:B0000YB8XO Music in the Weimar Republic]]<br />In 1970 he recorded the complete Bruckner 8th Symphony (his own edition) with the New Philharmonia Orchestra (in this box).<br /><br />Oskar Fried actually tied Klemperer - he recorded the 7th Symphony in 1924 with the same Berlin State Opera Orchestra: [[ASIN:B002HD1O5I The Art of Oscar Fried Vol. 4]]<br />He left no other Bruckner recordings, and has come down to us as a figure of mystery.<br /><br />Wilhelm Furtwangler left wonderful broadcast recordings of Symphonies 4-9 with the Berlin and Vienna Philharmonics: [[ASIN:B0012XIGZU Bruckner: Symphonies 4, 5, 6, 7, 8 and 9]]<br />Recorded in concert, 1942-1954. Unfortunately, he did not live to record in the stereo era.<br /><br />Hermann Abendroth recorded five symphonies with the radio orchestras of Leipzig (4,5,8,9) and Berlin (7).<br />Recorded between 1949 and 1956. Mono<br />Abendroth was overshadowed by his exact contemporary Furtwangler, who had the advantage of the Berlin Philharmonic.<br />As successor to Furtwangler and Bruno Walter, Abendroth conducted the great Leipzig Gewandhaus Orchestra from 1933 to 1945.<br />After World War II, the East German authorities banished him to the less prestigious Radio Orchestra of the same city.<br />[[ASIN:B00DANOMTO ãƒ˜ãƒ«ãƒžãƒ³ãƒ»ã‚¢ãƒ¼ãƒ™ãƒ³ãƒˆãƒ­ãƒ¼ãƒˆæŒ‡æ® ãƒ–ãƒ«ãƒƒã‚¯ãƒŠãƒ¼]]<br /><br />Carl Schuricht recorded Symphonies 3,4,5 and 7,8,9 in broadcast and in the studio, between 1938 and 1965.<br />Four were in stereo:<br />Symphony 3 (Vienna Philharmonic -1965), Symphony 7 (Hague PO -1964) [[ASIN:B0009I8EZQ The Concert Hall Recordings - Carl Schuricht]] , Symphonies 8,9 (Vienna PO -1963).<br />The three stereo Vienna Philharmonic recordings have recently been reissued in a nice EMI budget box along with his mono Beethoven cycle from Paris: [[ASIN:B008I15774 ICON Carl Schuricht]]<br /><br />Bruno Walter recorded Symphonies 4,7,8,9 and the Te Deum in broadcast and in the studio, between 1940 and 1961.<br />Symphonies 4, 7 and 9 were recorded in stereo with the Columbia Symphony - included in the new 39 CD Bruno Walter Edition: [[ASIN:B00BNZN27E Bruno Walter: The Edition]]<br /><br />Young Bruno Walter could be tough. &#34;Old&#34; Bruno Walter was warm and fuzzy. Musicians loved him.<br /><br />Otto Klemperer was not warm and fuzzy.<br /><br />I love Otto Klemperer's Bruckner, but it's not to everyone's taste.<br />He conducted with his fists and a scowl on his face, and the music sometimes sounded like it.<br />He stood an intimidating 6 feet, 6 inches tall, and had a reputation (deserved) for mental instability and irrational behavior (nowadays he would be called bi-polar).<br />His intimidating appearance was the result of surgery to remove a brain tumor, which left him partially paralyzed for the last 30 years of his life.<br />The irrational behavior was with him all his life.<br />Intentional or not, this had an effect on orchestra players.<br /><br />Following that 1924 recording, Otto Klemperer recorded Symphonies 4-9 in broadcast and in the studio, between 1934 and 1970.<br />The Te Deum was recorded by the BBC in 1961 and is on Testament: [[ASIN:B0006IY5JS Bruckner: Symphony No. 6; Te Deum]].<br /><br />EMI here offers the stereo recordings, 1960-70.<br />Symphonies 4-7 were recorded in warm Kingsway Hall stereo between 1960 and 1967, and are highly regarded by critics and public alike.<br />4, 6 and 7 have justifiably appeared in EMI's &#34;Great Recordings of the Century&#34; series.<br /><br />Clarity is guaranteed by Klemperer's old-fashioned seating arrangement - first violins to the left, second violins to the right - which was, after all, what Bruckner expected.<br />Not all violins scrunched together on the left, which is the modern preference.<br /><br />Klemperer's strings were seated in an arc: First Violins, Basses, Cellos, Violas, Second Violins.<br />You get a remarkable sense of being &#34;inside&#34; the orchestra.<br />Fun to listen to over headphones.<br /><br />Symphonies 8 and 9 were recorded in 1970: Klemperer was 85, the end was near, and the struggle shows.<br />The 8th Symphony has an enormous cut in the finale.<br />It was Klemperer's idea, and hard to justify.<br />His 8th and 9th Symphonies are clearly not standard recommendations.<br />Fascinating and quite moving, nonetheless.<br /><br />Klemperer conducted an uncut Bruckner 8th with the Cologne Radio Symphony in 1957: [[ASIN:B001VSBF6I Symphony #8]].<br />It times in at 72 minutes versus 84 minutes for the cut EMI recording.<br />72 Minutes is actually the fastest Bruckner 8th (Haas) on record.<br />Klemperer's manic-depressive pendulum had clearly swung to manic that day.<br /><br />I find comparative listening fascinating. My apologies if you don't share my compulsion. *<br /><br />REMASTERINGS: 2000-2004 EMI remasterings done in 24-bit resolution by Abbey Road Technology (ART) were used for Symphonies 4, 6 and 7. The other three are from 1990.<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /><br />P.S. A surprising omission from my list is Hans Knappertsbusch (1888-1965), the most &#34;old-fashioned&#34; of Bruckner conductors.<br />He was famous for sticking with the discredited Schalk and Loewe editions long after the earlier generation of Bruckner conductors had adopted Haas and Nowak,<br />but he wasn't as old as we think - He was actually the first of the new generation of post-WWI Bruckner conductors.<br /><br />P.P.S. B008YKRRI6|5.0|I recently came across this anecdote in Richard Osborne's biography of Herbert von Karajan:<br /><br />\\"[In October 1957] Karajan was off to Japan with the Berlin Philharmonic, though not before he made a flying visit to London to hear Klemperer conduct the Eroica Symphony with the Philharmonia.<br />It was a visit which led to a famous encounter in Klemperer's dressing-room after the concert:<br />KLEMPERER: Herr von Karajan, what are you doing here?<br />KARAJAN: I have simply come to thank you and to say that I hope I shall live to conduct the Funeral March as well as you have done it.<br />Good night.\\" *<br /><br />Three Nineteenth Century conductors lived long enough to leave us with a complete cycle of Beethoven Symphonies in the new medium of Stereo (beginning in 1956).<br />I'm fudging a little: Nineteenth Century Romanticism dragged on for a few years after 1900,<br />But their formative years were in the Nineteenth Century:<br /><br />- Bruno Walter (1876-1962)..................Debut: 1894<br />- Pierre Monteux (1875-1964)...............Debut: 1895<br />- Otto Klemperer (1885-1973)...............Debut: 1905<br /><br />Their conducting styles were different from each other (and from their younger selves).<br />Nevertheless, all three had strong personalities which they effectively communicated to an orchestra.<br />I still listen to these sets with pleasure, which is more than I can say for dozens of faceless conductors who came afterward (no names!).<br /><br />[hint: for ease of navigation, read the review though to the end, then come back and click on the links.]<br /><br />Monteux's Beethoven is the most \\"modern\\" sounding of the three - with the London Symphony and Vienna Philharmonic (distributed by Universal)<br />- As he aged, his interpretations also changed less dramatically than the other two: [[ASIN:B013HFUAHC Beethoven: Nine Symphonies]]<br /><br />Young Bruno Walter could be tough. \\"Old\\" Bruno Walter with the Columbia Symphony (Sony) was warm and fuzzy.<br /> Musicians loved him.<br />Included in the new 39 CD Bruno Walter Edition: [[ASIN:B00BNZN27E Bruno Walter: the Edition]]<br /><br />Otto Klemperer was not warm and fuzzy.<br />I love Otto Klemperer's Beethoven, but it's not to everyone's taste.<br />He conducted with his fists and a scowl on his face, and the music sometimes sounded like it.<br />He wasn't really a terrible old man - his intimidating appearance was the result of surgery to remove a brain tumor, which left him partially paralyzed for the last 30 years of his life.<br />This only complicated the problems caused by his life-long bouts of mental illness (nowadays he would be called \\"bi-polar\\").<br />He could be difficult to deal with, but the Philharmonia Orchestra and the British public loved him.<br /><br />Interestingly, according to contemporary accounts, Beethoven conducted his own music with his fists and a scowl on his face.<br />- And he really was a terrible man.<br />Can this be authentic performance practice?<br /><br />Klemperer's Beethoven has been reissued several times on CD, but this new collection is the first absolutely complete edition of his EMI Beethoven Symphonies, Overtures, Grosse Fugue, and excerpts from Egmont & Prometheus.<br />The Concerti, Missa Solemnis and Fidelio are not included - they are available in EMI's Great Recordings of the Century series.<br /><br />Two CDs worth of mono recordings from 1954-1955.<br />The rest is warm Kingsway Hall stereo.<br />Clarity guaranteed by Klemperer's old-fashioned seating arrangement - first violins to the left, second violins to the right - which was, after all, what Beethoven expected.<br />Not all violins scrunched together on the left, which is the modern preference.<br /><br />Klemperer's strings were seated in an arc: First Violins, Basses, Cellos, Violas, Second Violins.<br />You get a remarkable sense of being \\"inside\\" the orchestra.<br />Fun to listen to over headphones.<br /><br />The new box includes two recordings each of Symphonies 3 and 5, three recordings of Symphony 7, and two recordings each of the Overtures to The Consecration of the House,<br />Creatures of Prometheus, and Leonore 1-3.<br /><br />Also - for the first time on CD we have his late (1970) New Philharmonia recordings of Beethoven Symphony 7 and the Overture, Adagio and Finale from The Creatures of Prometheus.<br /><br />At their best, Klemperer's performances have a cumulative power unlike any other I know.<br /><br />REMASTERINGS: EMI has conscientiously given us the best available European remasterings (I can't vouch for Japanese remasterings).<br />6 CDs are 1998 remasterings done in 24-bit resolution by Abbey Road Technology (ART).<br />3 CDs worth of 2000-2003 remasterings from EMI's Great Recordings of the Century series (also 24-bit).<br />There is even 1 CD + of 2012 remasterings.<br />CD 4 claims to have used 1990 remasterings, but I am skeptical at least of Symphony 4: I have the 1998 remastering and they appear to be identical.<br />The 1960 Symphony 7 did not appear in the 1998 reissue series, so it may actually be the 1990 remastering.<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /><br />* Richard Osborne, 'Herbert von Karajan: A Life in Music'(1998), chapter 44.<br />This conversation took place two years after Karajan had achieved his lifetime ambition of being named Conductor-for-Life of the Berlin Philharmonic.<br /><br />P.S. Five other conductors from this generation also left us complete Beethoven Symphonies, but not in stereo:<br />- Felix Weingartner (1863-1942).............Debut: 1884<br />- Arturo Toscanini (1867-1957)...............Debut: 1886<br />- Willem Mengelberg (1871-1951)..........Debut: 1891<br />- Carl Schuricht (1880-1967)...................Debut: 1900 (symphonies recorded by French EMI, 1957-58, Symphonies 1-8 inexplicably in mono)<br />- Wilhelm Furtwangler (1886-1954).........Debut: 1906<br /><br />P.P.S. B008YKRRIQ|5.0|I first started listening to classical music when I was fifteen - in 1964.<br />My first big crush was Herbert von Karajan.<br />I think I may have been influenced by the sheer look and feel of 1960s Deutsche Gramophon LP records.<br />They were twice as thick as American records, came in extra-wide plastic-lined sleeves, and didn't snap, crackle and pop like American records.<br /><br />The infatuation with Karajan lasted about five years, until I discovered Otto Klemperer and Bruno Walter at my college record shop (what's a record shop?).<br />The American pressings were still pretty awful, but the music-making was revelatory: Walter was warm and comforting; Klemperer was imposing and majestic.<br />Still my two favorite conductors of the stereo era.<br /><br />Both men were dinosaurs: holdovers from the Nineteenth Century who lived long enough to give us stereo recordings.<br /><br />Bruno Walter made his conducting debut in 1894 and Otto Klemperer in 1905.<br />Their formative years were in the Nineteenth Century.<br /><br />Young Bruno Walter could be tough. \\"Old\\" Bruno Walter with the Columbia Symphony was warm and fuzzy. I find it invigorating.<br />Johann Strauss sounds like Beethoven.<br />The biggest surprise for me was how well Tchaikovsky responds to the Klemperer treatment.<br /><br />Warm Kingsway Hall and Abbey Road stereo.<br />Clarity guaranteed by Klemperer's old-fashioned seating arrangement - first violins to the left, second violins to the right - which was, after all, what these composers expected.<br />Not all violins scrunched together on the left, which is the modern preference.<br /><br />Klemperer's strings were seated in an arc: First Violins, Basses, Cellos, Violas, Second Violins.<br />You get a remarkable sense of being \\"inside\\" the orchestra.<br />Fun to listen to over headphones.<br /><br />REMASTERINGS: The previously issued 1998-2000 EMI remasterings done in 24-bit resolution by Abbey Road Technology (ART) are here (about 80% of the contents of the box).<br />One disc was remastered in 2011 (Schumann Symphonies 3 & 4).<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /><br />P.S. B0096HNDXY|4.0|I confess that I'm not a particularly devoted listener of Kylie Minogue's music. I've enjoyed her songs on the radio and web, and danced to them on nights out. But her music is usually a little outside the musical genres I spend the most time listening to (rock, jazz, blues).<br /><br />That said, what piqued my curiosity about this CD - and inspired me to give it a listen - was the very intriguing concept. The Abbey Road sessions features 16 of Kylie's songs, collected throughout her long career, completely reworked, and performed with a full orchestra and Kylie's band at London's Abbey Road Studios.<br /><br />Kylie's usual high-energy pop production has been scaled down (and the songs mostly slowed down) in favor of fresh orchestral and acoustic arrangements, sometimes hushed, some with soaring highs, and many with a jazz or blues flavor. All but one of Kylie's danceable pop hits are presented as ballads here. They're still mostly pop, but it's a more adult, sophisticated variety - often sweet or romantic, and occasionally dark or seductive.<br /><br />The album's first single, is the previously-unreleased &#34;Flower.&#34; Written during the time of her breast cancer treatments, when her eventual ability to have a child was in question, &#34;Flower&#34; is a wistful, tender love letter to a hoped-for future child:<br /><br />Distant child, my flower<br />Are you blowing in the breeze?<br />Can you feel me?<br />As I breathe life into you<br />In a while my flower<br />Somewhere in a desert haze<br />I know one day you'll amaze me<br /><br />&#34;The Locomotion,&#34; written by Gerry Goffin and Carole King, was a hit for Little Eva back in 1962, 25 years before Kylie's success with it in 1987. And, 25 years later, Kylie reaches back to the 1960s for inspiration, giving the song a fun Motown-esque makeover. It's the only non-ballad on the new album, and like the original may inspire you to put on your dancing shoes.<br /><br />&#34;Love at First Sight,&#34; a little more up-tempo than some of the other songs, is given a contemporary country twist. &#34;Hand on Your Heart&#34; is country-influenced as well. &#34;I Believe in You&#34; is a sweet love song. &#34;Slow&#34; is transformed into a sultry, seductive torch song; it has the feel of being sung in a smoky jazz club. &#34;On a Night Like This&#34; morphs into a stunning big-band number.<br /><br />As was the original, Kylie's new take on &#34;Where the Wild Roses Grow,&#34; is a duet with Nick Cave. This time around, it's given a traditional folk-style acoustic arrangement, which works well to highlight the story and emotion of this dark ballad about a man who takes the life of his trusting young lover.<br /><br />The packaging for the standard edition (I haven't seen the Deluxe) contains a booklet containing glamorous photos of Kylie, and lyrics for all the songs. The design is very elegant! However, if you'd like to read the lyrics or credits, you'd better have super eyesight or have a magnifying glass handy, because the text is incredibly tiny.<br /><br />While most of the songs on this album worked quite well in their new, more laid-back tone, a little more punch and sizzle would have better suited my tastes. But if you're looking for an elegant album with a lovely pared-down, contemporary pop vibe, featuring some beautiful vocals - maybe just the thing for cozy fall nights and the holidays - this might be the one.<br /><br />The Abbey Road Sessions caps a year-long celebration of Kylie's 25 years in music, which has also included the release of a greatest hits album, a hit single, &#34;Timebomb,&#34; and a global tour.<br /><br />It took some audacity for Kylie Minogue to reinterpret her songs in so radical a manner; this is a long way from anything her fans have heard, or expected from her before. But it mostly works - the album shows her versatility, and that there are exciting possibilities for her future as a performer. It will be fascinating to see what the next 25 years bring.<br /><br />Disclosure: The record company sent me an advance review copy of this CD. Rest assured that the opinions stated in the review are honest, and are solely my own!view are honest, and are solely my own!

B008RZF1UO|4.0|I was very excited for the release of Silverthorn. It really does mark a new era in Kamelot's history with Roy Khan's departure and his replacement, Tommy Karevik, being announced earlier this year. The Kamelot lads have done much since then to offer just enough tidbits about the album to create a steady groundswell of interest from fans, both those skeptical at a Khan-less process and those supportive regardless. I admit I was very much swept up by the momentum and I love a good concept album to sink my teeth into and try to match the story with the music.<br /><br />I took some time from my initial spin of the CD to pass judgment and attempt a review. I fully recognized that I was enamored by the hype and the prospect of a new Kamelot album and I wanted to give as rational and objective review as I could. Surely that's impossible, being a self-professed Kamelot fan, but I'll do my best. Had I written my review Saturday night when I got the CD, it would have been emotional, reactionary, and completely subjective.<br /><br />I miss Roy Khan. I can't help it. It's not fair to compare Tommy to Roy, since Roy did so much lyrically and vocally to form much of Kamelot's identity, but the reality is that Tommy will be compared to Roy fair or not. Karevik does sound similar to Roy, especially in his lower ranges, a fact that has not been lost on any of the reviews that I've read. Overall, I think Tommy is a phenomenal singer technically, but some of my criticism of Silverthorn is that Tommy is too clinically perfect in his vocals. They are almost a whitewashed performance. Roy had a wonderful way of intertwining emotion into his vocal performances...as if he were a method actor channeling the emotion felt by the protagonist in each song. I hate to say it, but much of that is lost in Tommy's performance. Where Tommy shines brightest is in the areas where his delivery focuses on the emotive over the technically perfect. Veritas, My Confession, and the title track Silverthornare all excellent tracks that are made so because Tommy's emotional voice makes the listener believe the feelings that underlie what he's singing. The same can be said for the three-part opus Prodigal Son, although I realize it's not for everyone. Other strong songs include the driving Ashes to Ashes, Torn, Sacrimony, and Falling Like The Fahrenheit, but they fail to live up to the other songs because Tommy's performance, while very strong, lacks that emotional punch to fully bring it home (at least for me). The weakest track is Solitaire, which incidentally is also the name for another Kamelot song on Ghost Opera. I find it repetitive, uninteresting, and containing rather clunky lyrics. Where I'm torn the most is on the balled Song for Jolee. It's a haunting soliloquy from a brother to his deceased sister. Again, Tommy's performance is technically very good, but here more than anywhere else does the album sound like Seventh Wonder rather than Kamelot. It's also a bit too close to the kind of Broadway musical soliloquy you would expect from Lloyd Webber than a song that would fit seamlessly next to the more up-tempo metal pieces. Despite that, I found myself trying to put myself in the brother's position and feeling moved, perhaps not least of all because my own daughter is five years old and would be about the same age as the sister when she died.<br /><br />That's as good a place as any to transition into the concept. I was drawn in by the background storybook included in the limited edition set. That being said, I think it's difficult to understand some of the lyrical clues in the songs without reading the story for context. None of the lyrics, for example, reveal exactly what \\"Silverthorn\\" actually is and why it's important to the story. The book, however, places it at the center of the entire narrative. Even with the book, it's important to understand that the songs are not narrative driven, that is they are not chronologically arranged to tell a story, but rather all draw conceptually upon the story from the book. It took me a few spins to realize that as I was trying to arrange the songs into some narrative that follows the written story. There's nothing inherently wrong with that approach, many a concept album has taken that tact, but it took me some time to get used to it.<br /><br />On the whole, while I think this is a very good entry into Kamelot's canon, there are areas that make me feel this album was rushed. For example, the first single Sacrimony has both Alize Ryd (clean vocals) and Alissa White-Gluz (dirty vocals) singing with Karevik. I had assumed that Alize's lines represented the voice of the dead sister in the brother's head playing almost the \\"good angel on his shoulder\\" while Alissa's vocals represented his internal pain, self blame, and doubt, or the \\"bad angel on his shoulder.\\" I think this would have been an interesting study of internal struggle to explore moving through the songs, but it evaporates after Sacrimony with only a couple of reappearances of the dead sister later in the album. It makes me wonder if the band toyed with the idea of a \\"voices in my head\\" motif and abandoned it. If not, I'm at a bit of a loss to really understand White-Gluz's presence in Sacrimony. On its own, I really like the song, but I think it establishes expectations that are never realized in the rest of the CD for whatever reason.<br /><br />The other place I felt the band rushed to production is in the packaging. Clearly, a lot of effort went in to conceiving the concept story and the conceptual imagery on the packaging to support the story. It's quite beautiful, actually, with a kind of washed out, wintery-blue feeling throughout. And yet, there are horrible misspellings in the printed lyrics. Prodigal Son is at the most fault here with multiple uses of \\"strenght\\" instead of \\"strength,\\" \\"taken to soon\\" rather than \\"taken too soon,\\" and, my favorite,  \\"Determines everybodies fate in life\\" rather than \\"Determines everybody's fate in life.\\" I hate to be the grammar troll here, but for all the careful planning and work that went into creating a visual atmosphere in the packaging, a thorough proofreading is not too much to expect.<br /><br />From a bird's eye view, I really enjoyed Silverthorn. It's got driving metal guitars, prog moments galore that add spice, and a good mix of symphonic orchestration and church choir vocals with the guitar work. Other reviews have pointed out that the mix feels very \\"compressed,\\" and I understand where they are coming from. At times, the bass seems to be lost in the mix, but I also think this was done deliberately to play on the melancholic, frigid mood that the story would like to evoke. It doesn't distract too much for me. On the whole, this is a very good Kamelot album and a commendable debut for Tommy Karevik. Despite some of the nit-picky flaws I've pointed out, I think the work taken all together rises above those flaws to present an enjoyable listening experience that should leave Kamelot fans looking to a strong future with Tommy at the helm. I just think he needs some more time to truly get comfortable and allow that to translate into the music.

B007TUAHVQ|5.0|Mega Bloks has consistently satisfied with me with their support of the Halo franchise, but throughout the years of buying a set here and there, one can't help but feel the childhood stigma of Mega Bloks being inferior to Lego just hadn't worn off... At least until this set.<br /><br />Mega Bloks has really upped the ante in quality with this and their upcoming Halo 4 sets and I can say with full confidence that I am definitely glad to have the Halo franchise in their hands. This set is roughly equivalent to Lego quality for what is easily half the price for a comparable number of Lego bricks - a real bargain for the amount of effort that went into it. The attention to detail and likeness to the Halo 4 Warthog redesign is enough to excite any Halo enthusiast, and if you weren't a fan of Mega Bloks before, now is seriously a great time to turn it around.<br /><br />The Warthog itself is a great piece, and the suspension on the tires allows for some pretty dynamic shelf poses if you've got some terrain for it to be riding over. The turret swivels a full 360 degrees (though it mostly only has 90 degree swivel with a Spartan on the gun, especially if you stick a weapon on his back) horizontally and swivels a little up and down, and the back of the hog shows off the updated Halo 4 design (that we saw in the latest Forza game) with all 4 gas canisters in tow - it even goes as far as to include the ability for you to have the back swivel down for loading a spartan in, which is a great feature you don't see in the Amazon pictures.<br /><br />Both of the Spartans included are modeled off of the same Mjolnir armor that Master Chief wears in 4 and are quite well painted in a small step up from previous Spartan figures (I say small because the Spartans have always been fairly well painted). The Elite figure is also based off an entirely new sculpt, and is considerably better painted and slightly more articulated than the previous Elites - he's a real looker, and I love his sleek design. 4 weapons are included with the set: The new sticky detonator from Halo 4, an Assault Rifle (seems identical to previous molds) painted a new black color, a green Spartan Laser (unsure if this had been in previous sets), and a carbine with a slightly more lavender paint than previous ones.<br /><br />In addition to all that, the set also comes with a weapon drop pod (looks like the ones that showed up in Halo 2) that has 3 different pegs for an assortment of weapons to be placed inside (it opens and closes, but can be a little hard to get used to opening) and a piece of Forerunner technology with adjustable folds laying on a medium sized desert plate. You can't really do a lot with it for play, but it looks really nice for display and makes a good position for the Elite to defend.<br /><br />My only small complaints are that it's a little hard to fit a Spartan in the passenger seat of the Hog given the tube that \\"powers\\" the floodlight (be careful not to lose the transparent piece when you dump out the pieces), I had to use a little bit of putty to keep the turret's pole tight in how it moves, and that although it's relatively easy to build, the packages all need to be dumped out at once so you end up with a lot of pieces to sort through (some people have had problems with Mega Bloks doing this - it's a minor annoyance to me, but I figured I'd get that out there).<br /><br />Overall, it's a REALLY great set (that comes with a decent number of bonus pieces), and if we're in for this sort of quality in upcoming sets (which we certainly seem to be given the previews), both Halo and Mega Bloks fans have a lot to be looking forward to. If you've thought Mega Bloks were sorta mediocre in the past, give them another chance, I promise you will NOT be disappointed. And, hey, if you get just ONE set, why not make it this one? It's excellently priced, it's well made, and it's the Warthog, man! You don't get more iconic of Halo than having the Master Chief driving orgunning Covies down in a hog.

And her hair? How the remaining actors were able keep a straight face throughout is a mystery. And she was ready to jump into bed with the first BBMF. Utter drivel with sub-themes of religion and immortality - oh and the master race. I've seen Tom and Jerry cartoons that have better and more logical dialog - and by that yardstick - Homer Simpson is a thoughtful genius. This movie is yet another sign of the decline of Western culture, aided and abetted by Hollywood greed. The visual effects were stunning in Blu-Ray, but the story went nowhere - pointless is one descriptive word that springs to mind; another is contrived. The Prometheus plot is what I would expect from a low budget horror movie - this one just has better special effects. Perhaps the disappointment that the movie ended before the story was over stems from a less than subtle attempt to set the stage for a sequel? If that is the case, the effective cost of this entertainment is at least double - not an acceptable proposition - a movie must stand on its own, or be marketed as a series. I could not identify with any of the characters. Perhaps it is too much of a stretch to believe that all the highly trained and carefully selected scientists and crew would act like incompetent fools - they seemed bent on their own destruction, without any rhyme or reason.

Is it really a story of redemption, though, of self-discovery, of purification of soul, of coming to terms with \\"who I am\\" through a greater understanding of one's place in the natural world?  Or is it journal-writing, describing the somewhat self-absorbed idea of \\"finding myself\\" while hiking long periods of time alone? For me, it is the latter. Strayed is not a naturalist or particularly spiritual so I found her lack of wonder and fascination with the \\"wild\\" around her a big down-side to the book. Not that she doesn't describe what she sees and hears [though often sounding like it's from a guidebook and not from the her \\"core\\"]. It's more about what she did or did not do every day and becomes quite repetitive and deficient by not taking the reader to a wider appreciation of her/our interaction with the flora and fauna she is surrounded by.  This takes a far greater depth of feeling and stronger writing style than Strayed was able to achieve.<br /><br />I was swept into the story, however, worried about her reckless tendencies to hook up with weirdos [sounds like she was lucky too many times and thus I hope young women are not flocking to the PCT alone to imitate this trek!]  She had one nasty childhood and can't seem to accept her mother's early death. She's basically a troubling tenderfoot [in  more ways than one] that the reader is truly concerned about.  The interweaving of flashbacks works up to a point and then the reader is ready for Strayed to get to the meat of why she's up there on the PCT and achieve some kind of nirvana.<br /><br />The book could have used more editing [as seems to be the case almost universally these days. 1407409395|2.0|\\"...if I agree to publish an idea like this...it'll kill your reputation. You're a Harvard historian, for God's sake, not a popshlockmiester looking for a quick buck.\\"<br /><br />\\"Renowned Symbologist\\" Robert Langdon was supposed to meet Jacques Sonier one night; instead, he gets called to the Louvre, the scene of Sonier's horrific death. Langdon spots clues to Sonier's murderer everywhere, which makes the French police officer, Fache, immediately think that Langdon is the murderer. Fortunately, sexy Sophie intervenes to shunt Langdon away. And then they are off, trying to avoid being arrested, trying to find the \\"Grail\\", and trying to discover who killed Sonier.<br /><br />NOTE 1: My French is atrocious; expect misspellings everywhere.<br /><br />NOTE 2: Light spoilers to follow.<br /><br />This book has been quite controversial. A lot of Christian and Catholic churches have been up in arms about the claims Dan Brown makes in this book--namely how Jesus married Mary Magdalene and how the Church will stop at nothing to try to conceal this \\"horrific and world-altering\\" religious conspiracy.<br /><br />Let's get this out right off the bat: I don't know if Jesus was married. Tradition has it that he was not, but since I wasn't there, I can't say for certain. All I know is that, if I learned Jesus was in fact married, I would probably be surprised, but I'm not going to suddenly abandon my faith just because of Jesus' relationship status. And while this may create a bit of controversy if it ever does appear to be true (I think there was a recent article that gave more evidence to this), I think a lot of people are just going to believe that it's all a bunch of lies, like they do with evolution. Good grief, stop making mountains out of molehills.<br /><br />\\"Controversial\\" non-controversy aside, this was an absolutely unintentionally hilarious book. Characterization is astonishingly bad, the pacing is dull, the clues somehow end up between too easy and too difficult, the mystery is snooze-worthy, and the narrator's over-the-top French accent sent me into gales of laughter.<br /><br />Our main characters are Gary Stu and Mary Sue--I mean, Robert Langdon and Sophie Noveau. Langdon is one of the most boring male protagonists I've had to read in recent history. I love how he supposedly looks so \\"bookish\\", with descriptions like this:<br /><br />\\"His usually sharp blue eyes looked hazy and drawn tonight. A dark stubble was shrouding his strong jaw and dimpled chin. Around his temples, the grey highlights were advancing, making their way deeper into his thicket of coarse black hair. Although his female colleagues insisted the grey only accentuated his bookish appeal, Langdon knew better.\\"<br />George Clooney anyone?<br /><br />It was painful how every character had to clamor on and on about his \\"accomplishments\\" and how \\"intriguing\\" he is, or how the ladies' panties get all wet when he enters a room. If this is not male fantasy, I don't know what is.<br /><br />The weird thing is, when it comes to actually solving the clues/mystery, Langdon is a moron. He spends so long talking about history, symbols, sacred feminine (I got a load on this one...), but he seems severely stumped when trying to solve a few clues. The worst was the mirror-image English. Do you seriously mean to tell me an American can't recognize mirror-image English? I, a measly engineer, experimented with reading mirror-image English and had no problems.<br /><br />Meanwhile we have the set of boobs, Sophie. Don't expect her to have a personality or purpose besides being a set of boobs to get captured. I think Sophie is held at gunpoint 800 times in this book. She is even dumber than Langdon, despite being a talented cryptographer. She cannot solve any of the puzzles on her own, even though her own grandfather wrote them.<br /><br />The rest of the cast was goofy and 1-dimensional. Fache might as well twirl a mustache for all the good he does. I had no clue what Arringarosa's purpose in the story was. Silas' Sally Sob Story was so overwrought and cliche, I was laughing at his self-flagellation. And Teabing...good God, Teabing. I have no idea how to classify Teabing. Other than I kept imagining Sir Elton John, and I'm not sure that was the \\"characterization\\" Brown was going for.<br /><br />But you know, a lot of thrillers are more about the puzzles and mystery and action than the characters. I can buy that. But you also know what? The puzzles, mystery, and action are horrible. The puzzles tend to be the same type over and over and over again. They also seem to somehow be way too complicated (such as translating one word into Greek, doing an anagram, then translating to English???) and way too easy at the same time. There really is no mystery; there are so few characters, that it's pretty obvious who the Teacher is about halfway through the book. And the action? PAH! None, or very, very sparse. Most of the book is long, boring talking scenes where Teabing and Langdon try to out-Wikipedia each other. I'd rather go to Wikipedia and read THAT then read the mangled facts here.<br /><br />The other thing that drove me bonkers was how EVERYTHING, from Walt Disney to Sir Isaac Newton, from playing cards to the Mona Lisa, was about the Sacred Feminism. I don't doubt that the Sacred Feminism had its influence, but come on! It's like Brown randomly threw in artwork or famous people to be a part of his stupid conspiracy theory.<br /><br />And the narrator! Good grief! A Geoffrey Harding narrated my copy, and he had the worst accents I've heard in a long time. Sophie sounded like a moron; the French sounded so ridiculous, I had to stop the iPod to laugh. And Harding reads everything in this movie trailer voice that makes the book seem so much more important than it is.<br /><br />If you want a smart, intelligent read, don't go here. If you are expecting Indiana Jones style adventure, I think this is just too slow-paced for you. If you are looking for a Buddy Read to snark over, you've come to the right place!!<br /><br />Brought to you by:<br />*C.S. Light*'s purpose in the story was. Silas' Sally Sob Story was so overwrought and cliche, I was laughing at his self-flagellation. And Teabing...good God, Teabing. I have no idea how to classify Teabing. Other than I kept imagining Sir Elton John, and I'm not sure that was the \\"characterization\\" Brown was going for.<br /><br />But you know, a lot of thrillers are more about the puzzles and mystery and action than the characters. I can buy that. But you also know what? The puzzles, mystery, and action are horrible. The puzzles tend to be the same type over and over and over again. They also seem to somehow be way too complicated (such as translating one word into Greek, doing an anagram, then translating to English???) and way too easy at the same time. There really is no mystery; there are so few characters, that it's pretty obvious who the Teacher is about halfway through the book. And the action? PAH! None, or very, very sparse. Most of the book is long, boring talking scenes where Teabing and Langdon try to out-Wikipedia each other. I'd rather go to Wikipedia and read THAT then read the mangled facts here.<br /><br />The other thing that drove me bonkers was how EVERYTHING, from Walt Disney to Sir Isaac Newton, from playing cards to the Mona Lisa, was about the Sacred Feminism. B008O69SZ0|5.0|One of the benefits of the demise of the music business as I / we used to know it is the end of the requirement of major sales to get a wide-spread release. Thanks to the web, sites like this, and so forth, most artists are no longer under the constant pressure to have \\"hits\\" and move 100,000 copies of something in order to get their music released and heard. As a result, artists like, say Ian Hunter, are able to keep making records (yes, I still call them that - sue me) and, even more, keep making the music they want to make without regard to whether or not they make the charts. I'm not really sure what \\"making the charts\\" even means anymore. Thanks goodness.<br /><br />And thank goodness for Ian Hunter. I have been a fan since the mid-1970's, my mid-teens, and am kind of astonished, really, to find that he is making what is probably the finest records of his career (Mott or solo) in the last 10 - 12 years. This, [[ASIN:B000068G2Q Rant]], and [[ASIN:B000NTPGCK Shrunken Heads]] are simply three of the finest records he ever made, ranking with the best of Mott, his first solo album ([[ASIN:B00080CO9W Ian Hunter]]) and [[ASIN:B000002Z7K You're Never Alone With a Schizophrenic]]. His writing is as cogent, trenchant, and pointed as ever, and in many cases more so. He and the Rant band rock as hard as he ever has and yet are as light on their feet as they need to be when such is called for.<br /><br />And the songs here are terrific, starting from the top: His string of great album openers remains intact with Comfortable taking pride of place alongside Once Bitten Twice Shy, Just Another Night, Still Love Rock 'n' Roll, hell, All the Way From Memphis and Death May Be Your Santa Claus, too. He also avoids the overly sentimental ballads that marred [[ASIN:B0028OF7P4 Man Overboard]], the one area in which his older songs tend to be stronger.<br /><br />It is a gloden age for music by talents like Hunter, John Hiatt, Dwight Yoakam, Niel Young, Loretta Lynn, and on and on - no longer at the mercy of mass tastes, they can make music that they really want to make and know that it can get to the people who really want to hear it. I really wanted to hear this and all evidence points to Hunter really wanting to make it. Hurrah.

B000GLKLCK|5.0|This is the best available set of Mozart piano concertos currently available, mostly because of the extremely distinguished playing of Murray Perahia.  The set includes the four earliest Mozart concertos, which are reworkings of pieces by other composers.  Also included are the 2-piano and 3-piano concertos (the 3-piano work is arranged for 2) in performances with the great Romanian pianist Radu Lupu.  The recordings are relatively consistent, even though they were made across a 13-year period in various venues and with different recording technology (analog and digital).  The balance between piano and orchestra is definitely weighted toward the soloist (almost all Columbia recordings were made that way) and this may upset some listeners.  Nonetheless, the contributions of both the English Chamber Orchestra and Perahia are ideal.  The set is spread across 12 discs, with several discs having short playing time.  A cheaper version of this set is now available, though the budget version does not provide any notes or documentation.
